class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Population_density_of_Africa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="681" data-file-width="738" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Population_density_of_Africa.jpg/220px-Population_density_of_Africa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Population_density_of_Africa.jpg/330px-Population_density_of_Africa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Population_density_of_Africa.jpg/440px-Population_density_of_Africa.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Population_density_of_Africa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Population density of Africa (2000)</div></div></div>\n<p><b>North Africa</b> is a region encompassing the northern portion of the <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">African</a> continent. There is no singularly accepted scope for the region, and it is sometimes defined as stretching from the Atlantic shores of <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> in the west, to <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> in the east. Others<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (January 2019)">Like whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> have limited it to top North-Western countries like <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, a region that was known by the French during colonial times as &#8220;Afrique du Nord&#8221; and is known by all Arabs as the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> (&#8220;West", <i>The Western part of Arab World</i>). The most commonly accepted definition includes <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, the 6 countries that shape the top North of the African continent. Meanwhile, &#8220;North Africa&#8221;, particularly when used in the term <i>North Africa and the Middle East</i>, often refers only to the countries of the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> and Libya. Egypt, being also part of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, is often considered separately, due to being both North African and Middle Eastern at the same time. North Africa includes a number of Spanish and Portuguese possessions,  <a href="/wiki/Plazas_de_soberan%C3%ADa" title="Plazas de soberan&#237;a">Plazas de soberan&#237;a</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ceuta" title="Ceuta">Ceuta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Melilla" title="Melilla">Melilla</a> and the Canary Islands and Madeira.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>  \nThe countries of North Africa share a common ethnic, cultural and linguistic identity that is unique to this region. Northwest Africa has been inhabited by <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berbers</a> since the beginning of recorded history, while the eastern part of North Africa has been home to the <a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">Egyptians</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>. Between the A.D. 600s and 1000s, Arabs from the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> swept across the region in a wave of <a href="/wiki/Conquest_(military)" title="Conquest (military)">Muslim conquest</a>. These peoples, physically quite similar, formed a single population in many areas, as Berbers and Egyptians merged into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_culture" title="Arabic culture">Arabic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_culture" title="Muslim culture">Muslim culture</a>. This process of Arabization and Islamization has defined the cultural landscape of North Africa ever since.\n</p><p>The distinction between North Africa, the <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> and the rest of the <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">continent</a> is as follows: \n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Nineteenth century European <a href="/wiki/Exploration" title="Exploration">explorers</a>, attracted by the accounts of Ancient geographers or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab_geographers" title="Arab geographers">Arab geographers</a> of the classical period, followed the routes by the nomadic people of the vast &#8220;empty&#8221; space. They documented the names of the stopping places they discovered or rediscovered, described landscapes, took a few climate measurements and gathered rock samples. Gradually, a map began to fill in the white blotch.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> entered the geographic corpus by way of naturalist explorers because aridity is the feature that circumscribes the boundaries of the <i><a href="/wiki/Ecumene" title="Ecumene">ecumene</a></i>. &#160;The map details included topographical relief and location of watering holes crucial to long crossings. The Arabic word &#8220;Sahel&#8221; (shore) and &#8220;Sahara&#8221; (desert) made its entry into the vocabulary of geography. \n</p><p>Latitudinally, the &#8220;slopes&#8221; of the arid desert, devoid of continuous human habitation, descend in step-like fashion toward the northern and southern edges of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a> that opens to Europe and the Sahel that opens to &#8220;Trab al Sudan.&#8221; Longitudinally, a uniform grid divides the central desert then shrinks back toward the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>. Gradually, the Sahara-Sahel is further divided into a total of twenty sub-areas: central, northern, southern, western, eastern, etc.\n</p><p>\nIn this way, &#8220;standard&#8221; geography has determined aridity to be the boundary of the <i>ecumene</i>. It identifies settlements based on visible activity without regard for social or political organizations of space in vast, purportedly &#8220;empty&#8221; areas. It gives only cursory acknowledgement to what makes Saharan geography, and for that matter, world geography unique: mobility and the routes by which it flows.</p><div class="templatequotecite">&#8212;&#8201;<cite><i>An atlas of the Sahara-Sahel&#160;: geography, economics and security</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></cite></div>\n</blockquote>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a> or &#8220;African Transition Zone&#8221; has been affected by many formative epochs in North African history ranging from <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> occupation to the Arab-Berber control of the <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Andalus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> As a result, many modern African <a href="/wiki/Nation_state" title="Nation state">nation-states</a> that are included in the Sahel evidence cultural similarities and historical overlap with their North African neighbours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> In the present day, North Africa is associated with <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">West Asia</a> in the realm of <a href="/wiki/Geopolitics" title="Geopolitics">geopolitics</a> to form a <a href="/wiki/MENA" title="MENA">Middle East-North Africa</a> region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> influence in the area is also significant and North Africa is a major part of the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_world" title="Muslim world">Muslim world</a>.\n</p><p>Some researchers have postulated that North Africa rather than <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a> served as the exit point for the modern humans who first trekked out of the continent in the <a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Recent African origin of modern humans">Out of Africa</a> migration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Geography"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Geography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#Definitions"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Definitions</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-3"><a href="#People"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">People</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-4"><a href="#Culture"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Culture</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-5"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Early_history"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Early history</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Antiquity_and_ancient_Rome"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Antiquity and ancient Rome</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#Arab_conquest_to_modern_times"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Arab conquest to modern times</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Transport_and_industry"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Transport and industry</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>North Africa has three main geographic features: the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> desert in the south, the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_Mountains" title="Atlas Mountains">Atlas Mountains</a> in the west, and the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile River</a> and delta in the east. The <a href="/wiki/Atlas_Mountains" title="Atlas Mountains">Atlas Mountains</a> extend across much of northern <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>. These mountains are part of the <a href="/wiki/Fold_mountains" title="Fold mountains">fold mountain</a> system that also runs through much of <a href="/wiki/Southern_Europe" title="Southern Europe">Southern Europe</a>. They recede to the south and east, becoming a <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppe</a> landscape before meeting the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> desert, which covers more than 75 percent of the region. The tallest peaks are in the High Atlas range in south-central Morocco, which has many snow-capped peaks.\n</p><p>South of the Atlas Mountains is the dry and barren expanse of the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> desert, which is the largest sand desert in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geology.com_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-geology.com-12">[12]</a></sup>  In places the desert is cut by irregular watercourses called wadis&#8212;streams that flow only after rainfalls but are usually dry. The Sahara&#8217;s major landforms include ergs, large seas of sand that sometimes form into huge dunes; the hammada, a level rocky plateau without soil or sand; and the reg, a level plain of gravel or small stones. The Sahara covers the southern part of Algeria, Morocco and Tunisia, and most of Libya. Only two regions of Libya are outside the desert: <a href="/wiki/Tripolitania" title="Tripolitania">Tripolitania</a> in the northwest and <a href="/wiki/Cyrenaica" title="Cyrenaica">Cyrenaica</a> in the northeast. Most of Egypt is also desert, with the exception of the Nile River and the irrigated land along its banks. The Nile Valley forms a narrow fertile thread that runs along the length of the country.\n</p><p>Sheltered valleys in the <a href="/wiki/Atlas_Mountains" title="Atlas Mountains">Atlas Mountains</a>, the Nile Valley and <a href="/wiki/Nile_Delta" title="Nile Delta">Delta</a>, and the Mediterranean coast are the main sources of fertile farming land. A wide variety of valuable crops including cereals, rice and cotton, and woods such as cedar and cork, are grown. Typical Mediterranean crops, such as olives, figs, dates and citrus fruits, also thrive in these areas. The Nile Valley is particularly fertile, and most of the population in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> live close to the river. Elsewhere, irrigation is essential to improve crop yields on the desert margins.\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions">Definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definitions">edit</a><span language">Spanish</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Western_Sahara" title="Western Sahara">Western Sahara</a>\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:right;">266,000\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:right;">538,755\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:right;">0.37\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><i>disputed</i>\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><i>disputed</i>\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><i>disputed</i>\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><i>disputed</i>\n</td>\n<td style="text-align:center;"><i>disputed</i>\n</td>\n<td><i>Disputed</i>: commonly <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> (Moroccan zone); commonly <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a> (SADR zone)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td colspan="10">Source: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_World_Bank" title="The World Bank">The World Bank</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_world_Bank_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_world_Bank-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table><div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<p>Variously the <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_Sahara" title="Western Sahara">Western Sahara</a> are considered to be part of the region <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_geoscheme_for_Africa#Northern_Africa" title="United Nations geoscheme for Africa">by the United Nations</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> while Western Sahara and <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a> (but not Sudan) are included <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_the_African_Union#North" title="Regions of the African Union">by the African Union</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> In general geopolitical and business usage, as for example with the World Bank, North Africa is often grouped with the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Acronym" title="Acronym">acronym</a> <a href="/wiki/MENA" title="MENA">MENA</a> ("Middle East and North Africa") and sometimes in American governmental usage the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geopolitical" title="Geopolitical">geopolitical</a> term <a href="/wiki/Greater_Middle_East" title="Greater Middle East">Greater Middle East</a>. Similarly, the traditional Arabic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toponym" title="Toponym">toponym</a> <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> (meaning "the West") is commonly used to mean the African part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab_World" title="Arab World">Arab World</a>, though usually with the exclusion of Egypt.\n</p><p>The inhabitants of the Spanish <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">Canary Islands</a> are of mixed Spanish and North African Berber ancestry, and the people of <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a> are of North African ancestry and speak a <a href="/wiki/Maltese_language" title="Maltese language">derivative of Arabic</a>. But these areas are not generally considered part of North Africa, but rather Southern Europe, due to their European-based cultures and religion.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="People">People</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: People">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/North_African_Arabs" title="North African Arabs">North African Arabs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">Egyptians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maghrebis" title="Maghrebis">Maghrebis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berbers</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Beduin_women.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2356" data-file-width="1519" decoding="async" height="264" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Beduin_women.jpg/170px-Beduin_women.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Beduin_women.jpg/255px-Beduin_women.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Beduin_women.jpg/340px-Beduin_women.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Beduin_women.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Women in <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The inhabitants of North Africa are roughly divided in a manner corresponding to the principal geographic regions of North Africa: the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile</a> valley, and the <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">Sahel</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> or western North Africa on the whole is believed to have been inhabited by <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berbers</a> since at least 10,000 B.C.,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> while the eastern part of North Africa or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_Valley" title="Nile Valley">Nile Valley</a> has mainly been home to the <a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">Egyptians</a>. Ancient Egyptians record extensive contact in their Western desert with people that appear to have been Berber or proto-Berber. As the <a href="/wiki/Tassili_n%27Ajjer" title="Tassili n\'Ajjer">Tassili n\'Ajjer</a> and other rock art findings in the Sahara have shown, the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> also hosted various populations before its rapid <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a> in 3500 B.C and even today continues to host small populations of <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_people" title="Tuareg people">nomadic trans-Saharan peoples</a>.\n</p><p>In the eleventh century, the <a href="/wiki/Banu_Hilal" title="Banu Hilal">Banu Hilal</a> invaded the North African plains and plateaus, but not the mountainous areas such as the <a href="/wiki/Tell_Atlas" title="Tell Atlas">Tell Atlas</a> range, the <a href="/wiki/Rif" title="Rif">Rif</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Aur%C3%A8s_Mountains" title="Aur&#232;s Mountains">Aur&#232;s Mountains</a> and brought with them <a href="/wiki/Hilalian_dialects" title="Hilalian dialects">Hilalian dialects</a> of <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>, which over the centuries have been in significant contact with other languages, including the <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Europe" title="Languages of Europe">languages of Europe</a>. They have contributed to the <a href="/wiki/Arabized_Berber" title="Arabized Berber">Arabized Berber</a> populations.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>The official language or one of the official languages in all of the countries in North Africa is Arabic. Today, the largest ethnic groups in North Africa are <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berbers" title="Berbers">Berbers</a> and <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africans</a>. The region is predominantly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jewish</a> minority in <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a> and significant Christian minority&#8212;the <a href="/wiki/Copts" title="Copts">Copts</a>&#8212;in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=North_Africa&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2017</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_North_Africa" title="Culture of North Africa">Culture of North Africa</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Biskra_market_1899.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5145" data-file-width="7014" decoding="async" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Biskra_market_1899.jpg/220px-Biskra_market_1899.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Biskra_market_1899.jpg/330px-Biskra_market_1899.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Biskra_market_1899.jpg/440px-Biskra_market_1899.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Biskra_market_1899.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Market of <a href="/wiki/Biskra" title="Biskra">Biskra</a> in Algeria, 1899</div></div></div>\n<p>The people of the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> and the Sahara regions speak <a href="/wiki/Berber_languages" title="Berber languages">Berber languages</a> and several <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Arabic" title="Varieties of Arabic">varieties of Arabic</a> and almost exclusively follow Islam. The Arabic and Berber languages are distantly related, both being members of the <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afroasiatic language family</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_languages" title="Tuareg languages">Tuareg</a> Berber languages are notably more conservative than those of the coastal cities.\n</p><p>Over the years, Berbers have been influenced by contact with other cultures: <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">Egyptians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vandals" title="Vandals">Vandals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Europe" title="Ethnic groups in Europe">Europeans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africans</a>. The cultures of the Maghreb and the Sahara therefore combine indigenous Berber, Arab and elements from neighboring parts of Africa and beyond. In the Sahara, the distinction between sedentary <a href="/wiki/Oasis" title="Oasis">oasis</a> inhabitants and nomadic <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouins</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tuareg_people" title="Tuareg people">Tuaregs</a> is particularly marked. \n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_stringer_bel_-_Ait_Benhaddou.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1146" data-file-width="1722" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Flickr_-_stringer_bel_-_Ait_Benhaddou.jpg/220px-Flickr_-_stringer_bel_-_Ait_Benhaddou.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Flickr_-_stringer_bel_-_Ait_Benhaddou.jpg/330px-Flickr_-_stringer_bel_-_Ait_Benhaddou.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Flickr_-_stringer_bel_-_Ait_Benhaddou.jpg/440px-Flickr_-_stringer_bel_-_Ait_Benhaddou.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_stringer_bel_-_Ait_Benhaddou.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Kasbah" title="Kasbah">kasbah</a> of <a href="/wiki/A%C3%AFt_Benhaddou" title="A&#239;t Benhaddou">A&#239;t Benhaddou</a> in Morocco</div></div></div>\n<p>The diverse peoples of North Africa are usually categorized along ethno-linguistic lines. In the Maghreb, where Arab and Berber identities are often integrated, these lines can be blurred. Some Berber-speaking North Africans may identify as "Arab" depending on the social and political circumstances, although substantial numbers of Berbers (or <i>Amazighen</i>) have retained a distinct cultural identity which in the 20th century has been expressed as a clear ethnic identification with Berber history and language. Arabic-speaking Northwest Africans, regardless of ethnic background, often identify with Arab history and culture and may share a common vision with other Arabs. This, however, may or may not exclude pride in and identification with Berber and/or other parts of their heritage. Berber political and cultural activists for their part, often referred to as <a href="/wiki/Berberism" title="Berberism">Berberists</a>, may view all Northwest Africans as principally Berber, whether they are primarily Berber- or Arabic-speaking.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Egyptians" title="Egyptians">Egyptians</a> over the centuries have shifted their language from <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_language" title="Egyptian language">Egyptian</a> (in its late form, <a href="/wiki/Coptic_language" title="Coptic language">varieties of Coptic</a>) to modern <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Arabic" title="Egyptian Arabic">Egyptian Arabic</a> while retaining a sense of national identity that has historically set them apart from other people in the region. Most Egyptians are <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni Muslim</a>, although there is a significant minority of Coptic Christians.\n</p><p>The Maghreb formerly had a significant Jewish population, almost all of whom emigrated to France or <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> when the North African nations gained independence. Prior to the modern establishment of Israel, there were about 600,000&#8211;700,000 Jews in Northern Africa, including both <a href="/wiki/Sephardi_Jews" title="Sephardi Jews">Sephardi Jews</a> (refugees from France, Spain and Portugal from the Renaissance era) as well as indigenous <a href="/wiki/Mizrahi_Jews" title="Mizrahi Jews">Mizrahi Jews</a>. Today, less than fifteen thousand remain in the region, almost all in Morocco and Tunisia, and are mostly part of a French-speaking urban elite. (See <a href="/wiki/Jewish_exodus_from_Arab_and_Muslim_countries" title="Jewish exodus from Arab and Muslim countries">Jewish exodus from Arab and Muslim countries</a>.)\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_North_Africa" title="Prehistoric North Africa">Prehistoric North Africa</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_North_Africa" title="History of North Africa">History of North Africa</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Due to the recent African origin of modern humans, the history of Prehistoric North Africa is important to the understanding of pre-hominid and early modern human history in Africa.\nThe earliest inhabitants of central North Africa have left behind significant remains: early remnants of hominid occupation in North Africa, for example, were found in Ain el Hanech, near <a href="/wiki/Sa%C3%AFda,_Algeria" title="Sa&#239;da, Algeria">Sa&#239;da</a> (c. 200,000 BCE); in fact, more recent investigations have found signs of <a href="/wiki/Oldowan" title="Oldowan">Oldowan</a> technology there, and indicate a date of up to 1.8 million BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The cave paintings found at Tassili n\'Ajjer, north of Tamanrasset, Algeria, and at other locations depict vibrant and vivid scenes of everyday life in central North Africa during the Neolithic Subpluvial period (about 8000 to 4000 BCE). Some parts of North Africa began to participate in the Neolithic revolution in the 6th millennium BC, just before the rapid desertification of the Sahara around 3500 B.C. due to a tilt in the Earth\'s orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sciencedaily.com_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-sciencedaily.com-24">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>While Egypt due to the early civilizations of Ancient Egypt entered historicity by the Bronze Age, the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a> remained in the prehistoric period longer. Some <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenician</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_colonies" title="Greek colonies">Greek colonies</a> were established along the Mediterranean coast during the 7th century BC.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity_and_ancient_Rome">Antiquity and ancient Rome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Antiquity and ancient Rome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Septimius_Severus_Glyptothek_Munich_357.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2409" data-file-width="1820" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Septimius_Severus_Glyptothek_Munich_357.jpg/170px-Septimius_Severus_Glyptothek_Munich_357.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Septimius_Severus_Glyptothek_Munich_357.jpg/255px-Septimius_Severus_Glyptothek_Munich_357.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Septimius_Severus_Glyptothek_Munich_357.jpg/340px-Septimius_Severus_Glyptothek_Munich_357.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Septimius_Severus_Glyptothek_Munich_357.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The first <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman</a> emperor native to North Africa was <a href="/wiki/Septimius_Severus" title="Septimius Severus">Septimius Severus</a>, born in Leptis Magna in present-day Libya.</div></div></div>\n<p>The most notable nations of antiquity in western North Africa are <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Numidia" title="Numidia">Numidia</a>. The Phoenicians colonized much of North Africa including Carthage and parts of present-day Morocco (including <a href="/wiki/Chellah" title="Chellah">Chellah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Essaouira" title="Essaouira">Essaouira</a> and <a href="/wiki/Volubilis" title="Volubilis">Volubilis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>). The Carthaginians were of <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenician</a> origin, with the Roman myth of their origin being that <a href="/wiki/Dido" title="Dido">Dido</a>, a Phoenician princess, was granted land by a local ruler based on how much land she could cover with a piece of cowhide. She ingeniously devised a method to extend the cowhide to a high proportion, thus gaining a large territory. She was also rejected by the <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Trojan</a> prince <a href="/wiki/Aeneas" title="Aeneas">Aeneas</a> according to <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a>, thus creating a historical enmity between Carthage and <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>, as Aeneas would eventually lay the foundations for Rome. <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Carthage" title="Ancient Carthage">Ancient Carthage</a> was a commercial power and had a strong navy, but relied on mercenaries for land soldiers. The Carthaginians developed an empire in the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, the latter being the cause of <a href="/wiki/First_Punic_War" title="First Punic War">First Punic War</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Romans</a>.\n</p><p>Over a hundred years and more, all Carthaginian territory was eventually conquered by the Romans, resulting in the Carthaginian North African territories becoming the <a href="/wiki/Africa_(Roman_province)" title="Africa (Roman province)">Roman province of Africa</a> in 146 B.C.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> This led to tension and eventually <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">conflict</a> between <a href="/wiki/Numidia" title="Numidia">Numidia</a> and Rome. The Numidian wars are notable for launching the careers of both <a href="/wiki/Gaius_Marius" title="Gaius Marius">Gaius Marius</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sulla" title="Sulla">Sulla</a>, and stretching the constitutional burden of the Roman republic, as Marius required a professional army, something previously contrary to Roman values to overcome the talented <a href="/wiki/Military" title="Military">military</a> leader <a href="/wiki/Jugurtha" title="Jugurtha">Jugurtha</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p><p>North Africa remained a part of the Roman Empire, which produced many notable citizens such as <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>, until incompetent leadership from Roman commanders in the early fifth century allowed the <a href="/wiki/Germanic_peoples" title="Germanic peoples">Germanic peoples</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Vandals" title="Vandals">Vandals</a>, to cross the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar">Strait of Gibraltar</a>, whereupon they overcame the fickle Roman defense. The loss of North Africa is considered a pinnacle point in the fall of the Western Roman Empire as Africa had previously been an important grain province that maintained Roman prosperity despite the barbarian incursions, and the wealth required to create new armies. The issue of regaining North Africa became paramount to the Western Empire, but was frustrated by Vandal victories. The focus of Roman energy had to be on the emerging threat of the <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Huns</a>. In 468 AD, the Romans made one last serious attempt to invade North Africa but were repelled. This perhaps marks the point of terminal decline for the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a>. The last Roman emperor was deposed in 476 by the <a href="/wiki/Herules" title="Herules">Heruli</a> general <a href="/wiki/Odoacer" title="Odoacer">Odoacer</a>. <a href="/wiki/Trade_route" title="Trade route">Trade routes</a> between <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and North Africa remained intact until the coming of Islam. Some Berbers were members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_African_Church" title="Early African Church">Early African Church</a> (but evolved their own <a href="/wiki/Donatism" title="Donatism">Donatist doctrine</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> some were <a href="/wiki/Berber_Jews" title="Berber Jews">Berber Jews</a>, and some adhered to <a href="/wiki/Traditional_Berber_religion" title="Traditional Berber religion">traditional Berber religion</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_pope" title="African pope">African pope</a> <a href="/wiki/Pope_Victor_I" title="Pope Victor I">Victor I</a> served during the reign of Roman emperor <a href="/wiki/Septimius_Severus" title="Septimius Severus">Septimius Severus</a>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arab_conquest_to_modern_times">Arab conquest to modern times</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Arab conquest to modern times">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kairouan_Mosque_Courtyard.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1530" data-file-width="2296" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Kairouan_Mosque_Courtyard.jpg/220px-Kairouan_Mosque_Courtyard.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Kairouan_Mosque_Courtyard.jpg/330px-Kairouan_Mosque_Courtyard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Kairouan_Mosque_Courtyard.jpg/440px-Kairouan_Mosque_Courtyard.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kairouan_Mosque_Courtyard.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Mosque_of_Kairouan" title="Great Mosque of Kairouan">Great Mosque of Kairouan</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, founded by Arab general <a href="/wiki/Uqba_ibn_Nafi" title="Uqba ibn Nafi">Uqba ibn Nafi</a> in 670, is one of the oldest and most important mosques in North Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Early_Muslim_conquests" title="Early Muslim conquests">early Muslim conquests</a> included North Africa by 640. By 700, most of North Africa had come under Muslim rule. Indigenous Berbers subsequently started to form their own polities in response in places such as <a href="/wiki/Fez,_Morocco" title="Fez, Morocco">Fez</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sijilmasa" title="Sijilmasa">Sijilmasa</a>. In the eleventh century, a reformist movement made up of members that called themselves the <a href="/wiki/Almoravid_dynasty" title="Almoravid dynasty">Almoravid dynasty</a> expanded south into <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>.\n</p><p>North Africa\'s populous and flourishing civilization collapsed after exhausting its resources in internal fighting and suffering devastation from the invasion of the <a href="/wiki/Banu_Sulaym" title="Banu Sulaym">Banu Sulaym</a> and <a href="/wiki/Banu_Hilal" title="Banu Hilal">Banu Hilal</a>. Ibn Khaldun noted that the lands ravaged by Banu Hilal invaders had become completely arid desert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cedid_Atlas_(Africa)_1803.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="8241" data-file-width="9873" decoding="async" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Cedid_Atlas_%28Africa%29_1803.jpg/220px-Cedid_Atlas_%28Africa%29_1803.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Cedid_Atlas_%28Africa%29_1803.jpg/330px-Cedid_Atlas_%28Africa%29_1803.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Cedid_Atlas_%28Africa%29_1803.jpg/440px-Cedid_Atlas_%28Africa%29_1803.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cedid_Atlas_(Africa)_1803.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1803 <a href="/wiki/Cedid_Atlas" title="Cedid Atlas">Cedid Atlas</a>, showing the Ottoman held regions of North Africa</div></div></div>\n<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> the area was loosely under the control of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, except <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish Empire</a> conquered several coastal cities between the 16th and 18th centuries. After the 19th century, the imperial and colonial presence of <a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empire" title="French colonial empire">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">the United Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a> left the entirety of the region under one form of European occupation.\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> from 1940 to 1943 the area was the setting for the <a href="/wiki/North_African_Campaign" title="North African Campaign">North African Campaign</a>. During the 1950s and 1960s all of the North African states gained independence. There remains a dispute over <a href="/wiki/Western_Sahara" title="Western Sahara">Western Sahara</a> between <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algerian</a>-backed <a href="/wiki/Polisario_Front" title="Polisario Front">Polisario Front</a>.\n</p><p>In 2010 &#8211; 2011 massive protests swept the region leading to the overthrow of the governments in Tunisia and Egypt, as well as civil war in Libya. Large protests also occurred in Algeria and Morocco to a lesser extent. Many hundreds died in the uprisings. This uprising is commonly referred to as the "Arab spring&gt;"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport_and_industry">Transport and industry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Transport and industry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="box-Refimprove_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=North_Africa&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br/><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a class="external text" remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tunis1960-040_hg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2621" data-file-width="4054" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Tunis1960-040_hg.jpg/220px-Tunis1960-040_hg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Tunis1960-040_hg.jpg/330px-Tunis1960-040_hg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Tunis1960-040_hg.jpg/440px-Tunis1960-040_hg.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tunis1960-040_hg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Thousands of people in North Africa depend on <a href="/wiki/Date_palm" title="Date palm">date palm</a> trees for a living. Tunisia in 1960</div></div></div>\n<p>The economies of <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> were transformed by the discovery of oil and natural gas reserves in the deserts. <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>\'s major exports are <a href="/wiki/Phosphate" title="Phosphate">phosphates</a> and agricultural produce, and as in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, the tourist industry is essential to the economy. <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> has the most varied industrial base, importing technology to develop electronics and engineering industries, and maintaining the reputation of its high-quality cotton textiles.\n</p><p>Oil rigs are scattered throughout the deserts of <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>. Libyan oil is especially prized because of its low <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> content, which means it produces much less pollution than other fuel oils.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=North_Africa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10"
